  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/705271-will-i-miss-anything-or-get-spoilers-for">Will I miss anything or get spoilers for any other Discworld books if I read this one completely out of order?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_705271'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F34511.Monstrous_Regiment">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/705271-will-i-miss-anything-or-get-spoilers-for">4 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/705271-will-i-miss-anything-or-get-spoilers-for">See all 3 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/36964682-mark-bright"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1416054316i/36964682._UX100_CR0,17,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/36964682-mark-bright">Mark Bright</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Actually, you might. Generally, I agree wholeheartedly with what the other guys say here, that it doesn't matter, BUT on this specific occasion I can <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Actually, you might. Generally, I agree wholeheartedly with what the other guys say here, that it doesn't matter, BUT on this specific occasion I can say that I read Monstrous Regiment a good while back having not read any of the others and really enjoyed it. Someone just saw it in a second-hand bookshop and thought the cover artwork looked cool so they gave me it out of the blue. As I said I really liked it. HOWEVER, I am now in the process of reading through (and absolutely loving) all of Discworld in order, a process begun in part by having read this book a few years back. So, I have just finished this book again, and have to say that I enjoyed it a lot more knowing more about Vimes, Angua and the Watch, Trolls and Vampires and just knowing that little bit more about lots of little references which whilst not essential to the story make it, I think, even more fun. So Yes and No! The brilliant thing is that I could re-read it as I have done and get the best of both worlds.<br />Ah, but now I see this question is a year old – sorry! You have probably read it by now and worked all that out for yourself. ☹<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1533779-why-is-monstrous-regiment-in-the">why is monstrous regiment in the industrial revolution section of Discworld series?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1533779'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F34511.Monstrous_Regiment">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1533779-why-is-monstrous-regiment-in-the">one year ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1533779-why-is-monstrous-regiment-in-the">Add your answer</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/4063615-hamilton"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_100x100-c7bfe730633c9983e27caaa66a8e244a.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/4063615-hamilton">Hamilton</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Because of it's timing (the AM Times plays a big role, but it is prior to the post office/rail road), and because of the general theme of modernizatio<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Because of it's timing (the AM Times plays a big role, but it is prior to the post office/rail road), and because of the general theme of modernization, lead by Ankh-Morpork, including acceptances of different groups including women, but also trolls and vampires, and the idea of military action modernizing. Also, because where else would you place it?<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>

Industrial Revolution, #3)</span></div><div class="reviewCta__controls" data-reactid=".25as6qsveo0.1.1"><div class="userRating" data-reactid=".25as6qsveo0.1.1.0"><div class="userRating__starsWrapper" tabindex="0" role="group" data-reactid=".25as6qsveo0.1.1.0.1"><button class="userRating__star userRating__star--mediumStars" title="Rate 1 star" data-reactid=".25as6qsveo0.1.1.0.1.$1"></button><button class="userRating__star userRating__star--mediumStars" title="Rate 2 stars" data-reactid=".25as6qsveo0.1.1.0.1.$2"></button><button class="userRating__star userRating__star--mediumStars" title="Rate 3 stars" data-reactid=".25as6qsveo0.1.1.0.1.$3"></button><button class="userRating__star userRating__star--mediumStars" title="Rate 4 stars" data-reactid=".25as6qsveo0.1.1.0.1.$4"></button><button class="userRating__star userRating__star--mediumStars" title="Rate 5 stars" data-reactid=".25as6qsveo0.1.1.0.1.$5"></button></div></div><button class="gr-button u-marginTopBottomXSmall reviewCta__writeButton" data-reactid=".25as6qsveo0.1.1.1">Write a review</button></div></div></div></div>

<div id="review_312984829" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/312984829" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/312984829?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060013165" href="/book/show/386371.Monstrous_Regiment">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=awesome-kickass-heroines">awesome-kickass-heroines</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=pratchett">pratchett</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=for-my-future-hypothetical-daughter">for-my-future-hypothetical-daughter</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=excellent-reads">excellent-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2012-reads">2012-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer312984829" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17055555202491315252">Pratchett addresses two questions here: (1) <i>
  <b>“Do you think it's possible for an entire nation to be insane?”</b>
</i> and (2) Does - and, more importantly, should - a <b>well-placed pair of socks </b> (for 'certain' sort of padding) make a difference?<br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380375143i/729643.jpg' width='200' height='140' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380375143i/729644.jpg' width='200' height='140' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><blockquote>
  <i>“The presence of those seeking the truth is infinitely to be preferred to the presence of those who think they've found it.” </i>
</blockquote> As far as Discworld series is concerned, this book can be easily read as a standalone novel. It brings us to the little backwards c</span>
  <span id="freeText17055555202491315252" style="display:none">Pratchett addresses two questions here: (1) <i>
  <b>“Do you think it's possible for an entire nation to be insane?”</b>
</i> and (2) Does - and, more importantly, should - a <b>well-placed pair of socks </b> (for 'certain' sort of padding) make a difference?<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380375143i/729643.jpg" width="200" height="140" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380375143i/729644.jpg" width="200" height="140" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><blockquote>
  <i>“The presence of those seeking the truth is infinitely to be preferred to the presence of those who think they've found it.” </i>
</blockquote> As far as Discworld series is concerned, this book can be easily read as a standalone novel. It brings us to the little backwards country of <b>Borogravia</b>, which is constantly at war with someone - this time it's with Zlobenia. Ankh-Morpork is expected to intervene, since, as Commander Sam Vimes points out in his cameo appearance, <i>
  <b>"the interests of Ankh-Morpork are the interests of all money-lov... oops, sorry, all <u>freedom</u>-loving people everywhere."</b>
</i> <blockquote>
  <i>"Borogravia was a peace-loving country in the midst of treacherous, devious, warlike enemies. They had to be treacherous, devious, and warlike, otherwise we wouldn't be fighting them, eh? There was always a war."</i>
</blockquote> Borogravia's economy is on the verge of collapse, and the country has pretty much run out of eligible soldiers. But this does not stop the little country, governed by a "probably dead" <b>Duchess</b>, the country that follows the ever-expanding ring-bound (makes it easier to add pages) gospel of the god <b>Nuggan</b>, who seems to spend his time coming up with new <b>Abominations</b> - recently, we've had garlic, chocolate, dwarfs, cats, oysters and the color blue join the ranks. <blockquote>
  <i>"So what we have here is a country that tries to run itself on the commandments of a god, who, the people feel, may be wearing his underpants on his head. Has he Abomined underpants?"<br>"No, sir. [...] But it's probably only a matter of time."</i>
</blockquote> Polly Perks does not care about war, money, or patriotism. What she cares about is saving her not-too-bright brother who has disappeared in the war. So, of course, she chops her hair off and joins the army disguised as a boy. (<b>Did I mention that lots of Nuggan's Abominations have something to do with women acting "non-womanlike"?</b>) A mysterious unseen benefactor advises her on the benefits of a well-placed pair of socks in the 'nether regions'. And given that the title of the book comes from the infamous <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_First_Blast_of_the_Trumpet_Against_the_Monstrous_Regimen_of_Women" rel="nofollow noopener">The First Blast of the Trumpet Against the Monstrous Regiment of Women</a>, the readers can easily guess the true nature of Polly's war comrades.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380375143i/729645.jpg" width="140" height="180" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380375143i/729646.jpg" width="260" height="180" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Like we have learned to expect from Sir Terry's novels, this book is witty and has plenty of funny moments. But, as many of his more recent novels, it is also sad, angry and frustrated with the events in Discworld clearly paralleling the things that make us angry in the "Roundworld". <br><br>I love that this book, like the rest of Pratchett's creations, makes me think. Some may complain that the message of this book is on the heavy-handed side, but I love it. <b>
  <i>I love that Pratchett is not shy or subtle about getting the message across about the evils and pitfalls of bigotry, prejudice, discrimination, and so-called patriotism bordering on jingoism.</i>
</b> He is not subtle about the things that people should NOT be subtle about, and I love it. He is open about asking uncomfortable questions and stirring up trouble, and love this as well.<blockquote>
  <i>“You take a bunch of people who don't seem any different from you and me, but when you add them all together you get this sort of huge raving maniac with national borders and an anthem.” </i>
</blockquote> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380375143i/729647.jpg" width="250" height="170" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380375143i/729648.jpg" width="150" height="170" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <p>Another thing I love about Pratchett's writing is that he is one of the (regrettably) few male fantasy authors <b>who can portray a woman as not a stereotype or a caricature or a mystery or an adorable nuisance or a far-fetched ideal</b> but as a normal (well, normal for Discworld), real, and believable human being.</p><blockquote>
  <i>“This was not a fairy-tale castle and there was no such thing as a fairy-tale ending, but sometimes you could threaten to kick the handsome prince in the ham-and-eggs.” </i>
</blockquote> As it's becoming common in Pratchett's novels, the ending is not happy but has bittersweet undertones. <b>There is an understanding that it takes a lot to change the world, to change people's beliefs and prejudices, that anything that is won is only a small step on a long road. </b> <i>"And now it wasn't that good things were happening, it was just that bad things had stopped."</i> Pratchett is not reluctant to show that the world's ways of dealing with things that are uncomfortable, that challenge the status quo is to pretend that they never happened, pretend that they are the exception rather than the rule, pretend that ignoring things would make them go away. <blockquote>
  <i>"We weren't soldiers, she decided. We were girls in uniform. We were like a lucky charm. We <b>were</b> mascots. We weren't real, we were always a symbol of something. We'd done very well, for women. And we were temporary."</i>
</blockquote>************************************************<br>This is now one of my favorite Discworld novels. <b>Five stars is not enough to describe how much I loved it.</b> And I adored the little cameos from the other Discworld characters, especially Vimes (I am a true Vimes girl, why'd you ask?)<blockquote>
  <i>"The enemy wasn't men, or women, or the old, or even the dead. It was just bleedin' stupid people, who came in all varieties. And no one had the right to be stupid." </i>
</blockquote><br>————<br>My ever-expanding collection of Pratchett’s Discworld reviews:<br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060202" rel="nofollow noopener">Guards! Guards!</a> <br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060297" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060304" rel="nofollow noopener">Thud!</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/157609988" rel="nofollow noopener">Lords and Ladies</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/160703976" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wee Free Men</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060281" rel="nofollow noopener">Hogfather</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/312984829" rel="nofollow noopener">Monstrous Regiment</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17055555202491315252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1461059727" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1461059727" />
    <a title="L.J. Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25376254-l-j-smith"><img alt="L.J. Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442452168p2/25376254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1461059727?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="L.J. Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="L.J. Smith" href="/user/show/25376254-l-j-smith">L.J. Smith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1461059727" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6210471259501810831">This is the second most-frequently read of my favorite Terry Pratchett books, the first being Night Watch, which helped get me through the death of my mother. My latest copy of Monstrous Regiment is so battered and dog-eared that it’s falling apart. From sentence number one I was riveted and if you are wondering whether you should read this book, the short answer is yes, turn your cursor right now to a new tab and order it, or even better jog out to your local (probably half an hour away) bookst</span>
  <span id="freeText6210471259501810831" style="display:none">This is the second most-frequently read of my favorite Terry Pratchett books, the first being Night Watch, which helped get me through the death of my mother. My latest copy of Monstrous Regiment is so battered and dog-eared that it’s falling apart. From sentence number one I was riveted and if you are wondering whether you should read this book, the short answer is yes, turn your cursor right now to a new tab and order it, or even better jog out to your local (probably half an hour away) bookstore (they’re an endangered species) and support them by buying it there.<br /><br />It’s that good. Sir Terry is that good a writer. Read the book before finishing this review; you’ll thank me for the advice, and if you haven’t already devoured the Discworld series you are in for the rollercoaster ride and treat of your life.<br /><br />By the way, Sir Terry is a personal hero of mine. When he died last March I cried for days. He went leaving the bravest self-written epitaph I have ever read: "Terry took Death's arm and followed him through the doors and on to the black desert under the endless night.<br />The End."<br /><br />I was so stricken and devastated when I read this that I wrote a short response to it, which I kept to myself because I realized that I had no right to besmirch Sir Terry’s courage by imposing my own end-of-life beliefs on his in public. I get choked up just trying to write about this wonderful man and I ardently look forward to the day when we can cure Alzheimer’s disease and save the minds of other brilliant people like him.<br /><br />And guess what? Monstrous Regiment is a “gender-bending” book, meaning that it’s about a girl who dresses up as a boy to get into the army of an extremely misogynistic and backward country so she can try to rescue her older brother Paul, whom she has taken care of since she was a child. That’s not a spoiler, by the way: that’s on the back cover. The entire story is about female and male roles, and whether or not they should change. Ahem. More about that later. But the point is, this is an extremely appropriate book for the first of my new reviews. It’s also a book that will probably make you think a few new thoughts, especially if female equality isn’t an obsession of yours, and therefore obviously worth reading.<br /><br />Monstrous Regiment will also make you think about war. There is enough about realistic fighting in it to make you freeze in shock at points. Like Night Watch, like all of Sir Terry’s best writing, it deals with a serious subject in a loopy way, and the loopiness in turn has a deadly serious razor edge to it. Yes, yes, war is bad and grass is green (except in drought-stricken California, where it’s dun) but Monstrous Regiment will make you feel up close and personal what it’s like to be a soldier. And heaven only knows they know what war is really like. If you don’t feel some wheels turning in your head as you re-evaluate your position on whether anybody should fight anybody with anything after reading this book you are either smarter than everyone else left here in the world (and maybe a bodhisattva) or dumber than a pigeon.<br />The third subject Monstrous Regiment takes on is religion. Yes, organized religion and heavenly religion, too. And, once again, the book will probably surprise you. It astounded and amazed me by its bravery in handling this particular hot potato. I won’t go into the subject at length here because there are some spoilers that are pretty important to the discussion, but suffice it to say that new thoughts may well rocket back and forth in your head at the speed of a light clacks’ signals, which is to say at c for celeritas. Sir Terry is a genius when it comes to turning established “truths” on their heads and then turning them back over again . . . and back upside-down just for good measure. <br /><br />So definitely, without doubt, read the book. There are some terrific characters introduced here. The protagonist, Polly Oliver Ozzer Perks, is a very likeable girl, and the question of whether she is a lovable girl—a hero to adore and/or emulate—doesn’t really even arise. I’d be entirely happy to have Polly on my team any day, and I’d not hesitate to call on her in time of need. She’s smart and solid. That’s high praise from me. <br /><br />Overall: The action in the book is fast and furious, the humor is hilarious, the writing is fresh and vibrant, the ideas breathtaking, and there are even a few brief appearances by Samuel Vimes, who is my favorite of all Sir Terry’s characters, male or female. That is my conventional review of Monstrous Regiment, and I give it a conventional five out of five stars.<br /><br />See the comments below for the rest of my review.<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="6210471259501810831" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer6024784057048558410"><br/>Five stars really aren't enough. <br/><br/>I think this might be my favorite Terry Pratchett book. I've read it at least 3-4 times, and re-reading it today, I'm delighted to discover that it's every bit as good as before. <br/><br/>As an added bonus, this book would be easier for new readers of Pratchett to pick up. There are a few characters from previous books, but they only have very brief cameo appearances. Other than that the book is pretty much self contained.... <br/><br/>pat<br/><br/></span>

<div id="review_1928947705" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1928947705" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1928947705?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1928947705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer63352932578786200">Yer mom wears army boots!<br/><br/>Men and women, women and men. Men without women, women doing just fine without men.<br/><br/>Terry Pratchett’s 31st Discworld novel, first published in 2003, is somewhat of a departure from the other series. We see Sam Vimes and some members of his watch and there are distant grumblings about Ankh-Morpork, but this is for the most part a stand alone about the small, proud truculent nation of Borogravia.<br/><br/>This struggling nation state is foundering after decades or centuries of warfar</span>
  <span id="freeText63352932578786200" style="display:none">Yer mom wears army boots!<br><br>Men and women, women and men. Men without women, women doing just fine without men.<br><br>Terry Pratchett’s 31st Discworld novel, first published in 2003, is somewhat of a departure from the other series. We see Sam Vimes and some members of his watch and there are distant grumblings about Ankh-Morpork, but this is for the most part a stand alone about the small, proud truculent nation of Borogravia.<br><br>This struggling nation state is foundering after decades or centuries of warfare, attacking and “defending” against the aggression of its neighbors. But Pratchett draws a picture of a population and culture bent and warped by a ridiculous religion and the near worship of a monarch. Blind faith and mistaken loyalties are two of the elements that Sir Terry has fun with in this very well done novel.<br><br>Told in a more lineal manner than many of his eclectic stories, this one has more a more somber tone than most, this being about war, but it also had some of the most laugh out loud scenes of any of his books. Perhaps Pratchett, taking on a serious subject, realized the need for some uproarious comic relief. His sense of timing is impeccable.<br><br>I always like it, whether in a film or a book, when a supporting character takes over and steals the show. While the protagonist here is a young girl who cuts her hair and surreptitiously joins the army, Sergeant Jackrum takes over and is by far the most interesting player.<br><br>This one took a while to get moving for me but then it really took over and I thoroughly enjoyed this. Pratchett asks some serious questions – in his enjoyably non-serious way – and leaves the reader with some thought provoking ideas about war and peace, about ourselves, and the value of tradition against common sense.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1548077674i/26950391._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="293" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="63352932578786200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_888734712" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/888734712" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/888734712?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060013165" href="/book/show/386371.Monstrous_Regiment">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer888734712" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer970037378279683751"><i> There was always a war. Usually they were border disputes, the national equivalent of complaining that the neighbor was letting their hedge grow too long. Sometimes they were bigger. Borogravia was a peace-loving country in the midst of treacherous, devious, warlike enemies. They had to be treacherous, devious, and war-like, otherwise we wouldn't be fighting them, eh? There was always a war. </i><br/><br/>The Night Watch goes to War! Or, at least, that was my expectation before I started the novel. Only thin</span>
  <span id="freeText970037378279683751" style="display:none"><i> There was always a war. Usually they were border disputes, the national equivalent of complaining that the neighbor was letting their hedge grow too long. Sometimes they were bigger. Borogravia was a peace-loving country in the midst of treacherous, devious, warlike enemies. They had to be treacherous, devious, and war-like, otherwise we wouldn't be fighting them, eh? There was always a war. </i><br /><br />The Night Watch goes to War! Or, at least, that was my expectation before I started the novel. Only thing is, this Discworld novel is not exactly about the Night Watch, even if Sam Vimes, Angua and a couple of others do participate in the conflict. The theme chosen by Sir Terry is indeed the lampooning of martial mentality, but the main actors are a bunch of fresh recruits in the army of Borogravia. Each of these recruits harbors a secret identity, and I guess it is not much of a spoiler to mention that for some of them that identity has to do with gender roles.<br /><br />A long history of open conflict with all its neighbors, in particular a recent attack on Ankh-Morpork clack towers has brought Vimes and the imperial forces to fight on the side of Zlobenia and has left Borogravia deprived of fresh blood to send to the slaughter. Recruiting parties are chased out of the impoverished villages empty handed, until Polly decides to switch genres and enroll. All it takes, apparently is a pair of socks, shorn hair and a lifetime of observing the habits of the opposite sex.<br /><br /><i> You might pick your nose a bit more often. Just a tip. Few things interest a young man more than the contents of his nostrils. </i><br /><br />The new platoon that Polly joins is heading for the front, where a defeated Borogravian army is dug in under the battlements of a massive fortress they just lost due to the deviousness of Vimes. Under the fierce tutelage of veteran sergeant Jackrum, a ragtag collection of losers and misfits is welded together into a formidable fighting machine, in the best tradition of war movies that Pratchett sets out to subvert with an energy and a self-assurance that proves the Discworld treasure chest of ideas is far for reaching its bottom. I spotted some clear references to Apocalypse Now and Full Metal Jacket, but there are probably more barbed arrows that I missed along the way. <br /><br /><i> I joined t'see exciting forrin places and meet erotic people! </i> declares Carborundum, a rumbling troll in search of adventure who probably means exotic people. The platoon also includes an Igor, an abstinent vampire, a pyromaniac, a pregnant girl, a religious zealot modelled after Jeanne d'Arc, a couple of runaways from a correction house, etc. Together they will become famous as the Monstrous Regiment and they will change the course of history.<br /><br />This would have been one of my favorite Discworld novels, given the mastery of plot, comedy and pathos that I have come to expect from every new Pratchett novel, but for a small bother. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ I enjoyed the reversal of gender roles, but I believe the joke was taken too far by the end of the novel, with almost everybody being a woman in disguise.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />An added attraction in the story is the second main avenue of attack for the author: religious intolerance. Borogravian military problems are compounded by their peculiar branch of belief in a living holy scripture, whose Book of Nuggan addendum is much longer than the main text and is updated weekly with new interdictions and sins, like red hair, pickles or singing. <br /><br /><i> The presence of those seeking the truth is infinitely to be preferred to the presence of those who think they've found it . </i> may not be the most original dictum written by Pratchett, but it is one I could subscribe to without reservations.<br /><br />Time to say goodbye to Pollyand wish her all the best of luck trying to revolutionaze the Borogravian rigid ways, while I add to my TBR the next Discworld issue.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="970037378279683751" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1064870845" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1064870845" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1064870845?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552149419" href="/book/show/399415.Monstrous_Regiment">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1064870845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15746388988469501390">Welcome to Borogravia.<br/>It is currently at war with Szlobenia. Well, Borogravia is always at war with someone. It has been at war with every single one of its neighours and with some of them even more than once. Why? Because they consider it their patriotic duty. Just like never giving up even if the only reason for their survival is that their enemy doesn't want to just senselessly slaughter them where they stand.<br/>One Borogravian doesn't really care about the war. All she cares about is finding he</span>
  <span id="freeText15746388988469501390" style="display:none">Welcome to Borogravia.<br>It is currently at war with Szlobenia. Well, Borogravia is always at war with someone. It has been at war with every single one of its neighours and with some of them even more than once. Why? Because they consider it their patriotic duty. Just like never giving up even if the only reason for their survival is that their enemy doesn't want to just senselessly slaughter them where they stand.<br>One Borogravian doesn't really care about the war. All she cares about is finding her brother. To do that, she cuts her hair, stuffs some socks into her trousers and marches off as a soldier in the army. She is surprised to find she is not the only woman/girl who had reason to disguise herself and enlist. Amongst the comrades in her regiment are a vampire, a troll and an Igor. To say nothing of the apparently legendary Sergeant Jackrum. <br>On the periphery, we get a few glimpses at this mess from the POV of Commander Vimes and deWord as this business is involving Ankh-Morpork, too (so long as they can help an underdog against a royal without having to spend too much money).<br> <br>Sir Terry obviously had quite a few things to say about war and patriotism, corporate and state interests in wars and general soldiering. It might very well be that he and I had quite different opinions on the matter(s). Then again, it's entirely possible that we might have agreed at least on some of the things. It's hard to say since Sir Terry isn't just repeating old jokes and snide remarks (the ones about soldiers being too stupid to be doing any other job, or the ones of officers vs. grunts etc). That would be too one-sided and narrow-minded. Instead, he was aware of and also showed (commended even) the other, more sensible aspects (the camaraderie for example). And that is what I love so bloody much about the author: no matter his personal stance, he always gives a 360°-view. This is important since nothing, especially not something as complex as state politics and wars, is black-or-white. It's not just one thing or another either. To get to the bottom of it, to be able to form an informed opinion, you need all available information and he didn't just want to make you agree with him - he wanted to make you think for yourself.<br> <br>As far as I can tell, this installment of the <i>Discworld</i> series was published after the start of the second Iraq war. But while that might have been the reason for him writing this book, Sir Terry is settling the account with humanity's overall history of war. Thus, the story has elements from the Bosnian war, the two World Wars, perhaps even the Falkland wars as well. To give this book an additional edge (because that is how the author rolled), the story is also about gender roles, religious fanatism and the concepts of honour and freedom.<br> <br>Delivering the necessary comic relief are the quirks of all the different races in the titular Monstrous Regiment - including but not limited to the vampire's coffee obsession - and the general absurdity of many a situation. The most endearing bit (to me) was the heart-warming <s>sister</s>brotherhood of the regiment.<br> <br>Not the strongest installment of the series but seriously good in its own right.<br> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1576003155i/28591254._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="15746388988469501390" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1092785043" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1092785043" />
    <a title="Melki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5293536-melki"><img alt="Melki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308071306p2/5293536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1092785043?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melki" href="/user/show/5293536-melki">Melki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060013165" href="/book/show/386371.Monstrous_Regiment">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=humorous-fiction">humorous-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=war">war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1092785043" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3241999760035829206"><b>That'd been almost a year ago, when any recruiting party that came past went away with the best part of a battalion, and there had been people waving them off with flags and music. Sometimes, now, smaller parties of men came back. The lucky ones were missing only one arm or one leg. There were no flags.</b><br/><br/>It's hard finding soldiers when you're always at war. Especially when you're fighting a war that you may not be winning.<br/><br/>Eventually, you end up taking whatever you can get...trolls, vampires, even.</span>
  <span id="freeText3241999760035829206" style="display:none"><b>That'd been almost a year ago, when any recruiting party that came past went away with the best part of a battalion, and there had been people waving them off with flags and music. Sometimes, now, smaller parties of men came back. The lucky ones were missing only one arm or one leg. There were no flags.</b><br /><br />It's hard finding soldiers when you're always at war. Especially when you're fighting a war that you may not be winning.<br /><br />Eventually, you end up taking whatever you can get...trolls, vampires, even...GASP!...women!<br /><br />The horror, the horror!<br /><br />The newest recruits to This Man's Army (snicker!) are a mixed bunch and they're quickly discovering that war is not all it's cracked up to be. They find themselves ill-equipped with only hand-me-downs from dead soldiers and no rations.<br /><br /><b>"We have to <i>steal</i> our food?" said Maladict.<br /><br />"No, you can starve if that takes your fancy," said the corporal. "I've starved a few times. There's no future in it."</b><br /><br />Things can only get worse. Then, they do.<br /><br /><b>"Men, I have good news," he added, but in the voice of one who hasn't. "You were probably expecting a week or two in the training camp in Crotz, yes? But I'm glad to be able to tell you that the, the war is progressing so, so <i>well</i> that you are to go directly to the front."</b><br /><br />Soon, reality sets in and the realization of their dire predicament descends like a fog.<br /><br /><b>They were losing the war. Everyone knew that, but nobody would say it. It was as if they felt that if the words weren't said out loud then it wasn't really happening. They were losing the war and this squad, untrained and untried, fighting in dead men's boots, could only help them lose it faster.</b><br /><br />I have a problem when it comes to some Pratchett novels, particularly those lacking in witches. I'm reading along like gangbusters, then about halfway through, I lose all interest. I begin eyeing up that stack of unread, perhaps, <i>more</i> interesting books by the bed. I call this "The Discworld Doldrums." Luckily, my attention bounced back with this one, though I honestly believe a pared down version might have been funnier. So, this was definitely not a high point in the series for me. HOWEVER - Pratchett does a fantastic job of summing up the folly of war.<br /><br /><b>"We are a proud country."<br /><br />"What are you proud of?"<br /><br />It came swiftly, like a blow, and Polly realized how wars happened. You took that shock that had run through her and let it boil.<br /><br /><i>...it may be corrupt, benighted, and stupid, but it's ours...<br />We have our pride. And that's what we're proud of. We're proud of being proud...</i></b><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3241999760035829206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9670134653406153130">It is an amazing coincidence that my 31st Discworld novel is #31 since I have focused on the City Watch series, the Tiffany Aching series, and the recent, more humorous books.<br/><br/>In this one, Sir Terry's prime focus on the follies of war.   <span id="freeText9670134653406153130" style="display:none">It is an amazing coincidence that my 31st Discworld novel is #31 since I have focused on the City Watch series, the Tiffany Aching series, and the recent, more humorous books.<br /><br />In this one, Sir Terry's prime focus on the follies of war. The aging but much experienced Sergeant Jackrum is recruiting soldiers because most have been captured or killed, even though his side is supposedly winning. Only misfits are left -- those wanting to escape their dreary or worse lives. So, he ends up with a vampire, a troll, an Igor, and a young woman, named Polly, who pretends to be a boy (Private Oliver Perks) so she can find her brother, who is MIA. Leading the group is Lieutenant Blouse, a thinking officer, who is derisively referred to as a Rupert. Against all odds, this group <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[of all women, except Blouse <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> manages to change the course of the war with their bravery, determination and common sense.<br /><br />Favorite quote: <i>The enemy wasn't men, or women, or the old, or even the dead. It was just bleedin' stupid people, who came in all varieties. And no one had the right to be stupid.</i> </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2496295583" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2496295583" />
    <a title="Mareike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/85484632-mareike"><img alt="Mareike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578868215p2/85484632.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2496295583?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mareike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mareike" href="/user/show/85484632-mareike">Mareike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/85484632-mareike?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/85484632-mareike?shelf=2020-tbr">2020-tbr</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2496295583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16727669601011229182">3.5 stars rounded up to 4<br/><br/>I audibly snorted more times while reading this book than I did during all my other reads this year combined.<br/><br/>Pratchett tackles a lot of things in this book – nationalism, war, religion (including civili religion) and how it can be instrumentalized, the Army, and gender (especially gender equality) and with most of these, especially when tackling war, the Army, and nationalism, his humor and satire are outstanding in the best Pratchett manner.<br/><br/>At the same time, some aspect</span>
  <span id="freeText16727669601011229182" style="display:none">3.5 stars rounded up to 4<br /><br />I audibly snorted more times while reading this book than I did during all my other reads this year combined.<br /><br />Pratchett tackles a lot of things in this book – nationalism, war, religion (including civili religion) and how it can be instrumentalized, the Army, and gender (especially gender equality) and with most of these, especially when tackling war, the Army, and nationalism, his humor and satire are outstanding in the best Pratchett manner.<br /><br />At the same time, some aspects of the book didn't quite land for me. I'll put the rest of this review under spoiler tags since I'm going to go into detail and will be quoting parts that happen towards the end of the book. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[This is a book about women enlisting in the Army for a variety of reasons (finding a brother, running away from dire circumstances, finding a “husband”, looking for a way to prove one’s skills, etc.) and it’s here where the novel sometimes didn’t quite land for me.<br /><br />I think it is fair to say that when it comes to gender, Pratchett was mostly concerned with <i>gender equality</i> and the structural issues that lead to gender <i>inequality</i> not so much with gender identity (though these two things can of course not be separated completely). The novel is definitely completely on point when, after the reveal that a big part of the Army’s leadership, including the famed General Froc, are actually women, it highlights that an institution like the Military isn’t going to magically transform just because women enter it. Rather, the institutional structures will change the individual – especially if that individual wants to rise in the ranks. <br /><br />At the same time, the repeated scenes of revelation throughout the book are also where things got a bit sticky for me. I’ve thought long about how to put this into words, but I think the easiest way of saying it is that the book deals with gender from a very cisgender and heterosexual perspective. While there are some passages that deal quite well with gender performativity and the interdependency between presentation and how the world around people will treat them based on that presentation, the book also and with few exceptions, starts to use she/her pronouns (and sometimes the characters former name) as soon as a character is revealed as female. There are a few exceptions, for example Sergeant Jackrum and General Froc and with the latter, the use of “he” and the title often comes with a nudge and a wink after the reveal. All of this drives home that, in the eyes of the narrator, the characters are all really women, no matter for how long they have been living as men. <br /><br />Mostly, I think, this is due to the position from which Pratchett was writing this and, again, on what is focus usually is. In addition, he is clearly drawing on some well-known stories of women who enlisted in the Armed Forces as men and the reasons that were given in those stories. Deborah Sampson/Robert Shurtlieff is an obvious example from U.S.-American history. In the narrative about Sampson/Shurtlieff’s life – written by Massachusett’s writer Herman Mann – patriotism is highlighted as a motif for enlistment. However, as scholars like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14370010.Jen_Manion" title="Jen Manion" rel="nofollow noopener">Jen Manion</a> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51771013.Female_Husbands_A_Trans_History" title="Female Husbands A Trans History by Jen Manion" rel="nofollow noopener">Female Husbands: A Trans History</a>) and <a href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/25057161" rel="nofollow noopener">Judith Hiltner</a> have pointed out, these kinds of narratives often draw heavily on already existing texts about “female husbands” and “female soldiers” and highlighting patriotism was one way of making the transgression of fairly fixed gender lines palatable to contemporary audiences. It makes sense that Pratchett is drawing on these stories for his characters’ backstories, but in its insistence that all these characters are “really women” the novel also forecloses the possibility that some of them might actually be trans or enby. <br /><br />This also means that some of the jokes come very close to common transphobic stereotypes. This is especially notable when Lieutenant Blouse (one of the few cisgender men) dresses up as a washerwoman to sneak into a castle. What Pratchett is drawing on in those sections is clearly the tradition of young men playing female characters in Renaissance theater and also the tradition of drag in the Army, but a lot of the humor turns on the “a man in a dress who is badly imitating a woman” trope which also has a decidedly transphobic undercurrent. I think Pratchett was probably not aware of that undercurrent (I wouldn’t have picked up on it had I read this book when it was first published, or even 5 years ago), so I’m giving him the benefit of the doubt here. (I wrestled a bit with whether I was too ready to do that for Pratchett compared to some other very famous fantasy author who shall not be named, but since Pratchett didn’t have a habit of making increasingly transphobic statements (afaik) and since some of the issues I had with the book clearly come from his focus on structures of inequality and from him drawing on commonly known historical sources about women in the Army/drag in the Army, and the history of theater, I do feel justified.)<br /><br />All of this being said, there are two characters, one pivotal, one who is only mentioned in passing a few times, that could be read as trans, so I want to talk about them for a moment.<br />Sergeant Jackrum is not only the character who reveals that part of the Army’s leadership is female, but also the sergeant of the eponymous regiment. And, as becomes clear during a conversation between Perks and Jackrum is also passing. (There are hints before this moment, but this is when the novel makes it clear.) And while the text does continue using his last name and he/him pronouns afterwards, this only happens after the following exchange:<br /><br /><i><blockquote> “Wouldn’t wish meself on him, lad,” said Jackrum firmly. “Wouldn’t dare. My boy’s a well-respected man in the town! What’ve I got to offer? He’ll not want some fat ol’ biddy banging on his back door and gobbing baccy juice all over the place and telling him she’s his mother!” <br />Polly looked at the fire for a moment, and felt the idea creep into her mind. <br />“What about a distinguished-looking sergeant major, shiny with braid, loaded with medals, arriving at the front door in a grand coach and telling him he’s his father?” she said. <br />Jackrum stared. <br />“Tides of war, and all that,” Polly went on, mind suddenly racing. “Young love. Duty calls. Families scattered. Hopeless searching. Decades pass. Fond memories. Then…oh, an overheard conversation in a bar, yeah, that’d work. Hope springs. A new search. Greasing palms. The recollections of old women. At last, an address—” <br />“What’re you saying, Perks?” <br />“You’re a liar, Sarge,” said Polly, leaning forward. “Best I’ve ever heard. One last lie pays for all! Why not? You could show him the locket. You could tell him about the girl you left behind you…”</blockquote></i><br /><br />This exchange, and especially Perks’ insistence that Jackrum is a liar and can continue to live that lie if it means being reunited with family highlights that enlisting in the military while passing as a man, in the world of this novel, is always an act, a lie, never an expression of trans identity. And, again, I can see why Pratchett went this way, but it would have been nice to have at least one trans character.<br /><br />The other character mentioned above is Captain Wrigglesworth, a schoolmate of Lieutenant Blouses who, as several characters mention was <i>“Keen on, er—” “Amateur dramatics,”</i>. Here, again, the novel nods to the tradition of drag in the Army, but there are also a few moments where Lt. Blouse, for example, stops and seems to have second thoughts about whether “amateur dramatics” are the only explanation of Wrigglesworth’s talent at passing for a woman. This is never elaborated further, so while one can read this as a hint at a possible trans character in this universe, it fell…..a bit short. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />TL;DR I enjoyed this novel, but there are some things in terms of how gender identity is represented that I wish Pratchett had done differently. (I’m not saying he’s a bad person because he didn’t, I can see why and how the novel turned out how it did. This is probably mostly a “me thing” thing and not a “him thing”. (Reading novels that are very close to one’s main research area can be tricky in this way.))<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16727669601011229182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_894010891" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/894010891?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meghan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meghan" href="/user/show/9003269-meghan">Meghan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780060013165" href="/book/show/386371.Monstrous_Regiment">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9003269-meghan?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9003269-meghan?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer894010891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18404631380052592406">Ahh.. thank goodness for GR reviews.<br/><br/>Why, you ask?<br/><br/>Because I'm about to besmirch an author I adore. And I'm glad to be following in other footsteps that had a similar hill to conquer.<br/><br/>I don't love this book. I just don't. Mr. Pratchett has a gentle and funny way of adding real world elements to his books, poking fun at some pretty difficult moral issues. And yet this book seemed too heavy-handed, too obvious, and too repetitive.<br/><br/>Once upon a time I considered myself a feminist. That was before husban</span>
  <span id="freeText18404631380052592406" style="display:none">Ahh.. thank goodness for GR reviews.<br /><br />Why, you ask?<br /><br />Because I'm about to besmirch an author I adore. And I'm glad to be following in other footsteps that had a similar hill to conquer.<br /><br />I don't love this book. I just don't. Mr. Pratchett has a gentle and funny way of adding real world elements to his books, poking fun at some pretty difficult moral issues. And yet this book seemed too heavy-handed, too obvious, and too repetitive.<br /><br />Once upon a time I considered myself a feminist. That was before husband and children came along and biology reminded me that I had a certain role to play (instinct is like that). I take pride in being female, I respect and adore men for what they are. Both genders are equal- equal here meaning 'having gifts deserving of identical weight', NOT equal-meaning-the-same. I love that about us. And it means I can appreciate how men and women can be the yin and yang of society. Both different, both important. And I'll add a dirty little secret: I really don't mind reading books with a male lead. It honestly doesn't make me feel victimized. Maybe that affected my reading.<br /><br />I felt that this book was written for a highly feminist set, which would have been fine.. but it just takes the entire thing much much too far. I can buy certain aspects of it- so a war-torn country has no sons, and an entire regiment is made up of women. 'K. I'll take it. But the book doesn't stop there. And two reveals in this book bothered me completely: they would have added the humor factor to the book if they had been left as they were instead. That reminded me of another Pratchett book- one I loved intensely- where a character commits suicide. I so wanted her to pop back up and say 'Well, that didn't work', and laugh along. But she didn't... because the author had a statement to make, instead. Just like in this book. <br /><br />Terry Pratchett writes incredible female characters, anyway, and he did equally well with this one (missing only one monthly issue that he may not have wanted to discuss- but at one point in the book I thought it was going to be that moment.. nope, just socks ;) ). There were funny aspects here, too. But when all was said and done, it felt like a moral lesson rather than a jolly romp through the picker bushes of humanity.<br /><br />Can't bring myself to give him less than 4 stars for, once again, incredible characters, writing, and story flow. But I didn't love it. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18404631380052592406" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2150107862" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2150107862" />
    <a title="Michael Campbell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72416163-michael-campbell"><img alt="Michael Campbell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583366142p2/72416163.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2150107862?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Campbell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Campbell" href="/user/show/72416163-michael-campbell">Michael Campbell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2150107862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4709547090575737606">This is my third time reading this book, and I've decided that the few sentences I wrote last time were not nearly enough to describe my thoughts and feelings towards this book. This is one of the most genuinely funny and thought provoking books I've ever picked up. I've been thinking about it a lot lately in regard to it's thoughts on nationalism, and I often think the world would be a better place if more people read Discworld.<br/><br/>Nationalistic pride is just one topic discussed here, however. It's</span>
  <span id="freeText4709547090575737606" style="display:none">This is my third time reading this book, and I've decided that the few sentences I wrote last time were not nearly enough to describe my thoughts and feelings towards this book. This is one of the most genuinely funny and thought provoking books I've ever picked up. I've been thinking about it a lot lately in regard to it's thoughts on nationalism, and I often think the world would be a better place if more people read Discworld.<br /><br />Nationalistic pride is just one topic discussed here, however. It's just the one most on my mind at present, but this book is full of insightful knowledge. There are few writers in history who could make shoving a sock down the front of one's pants philosophic, but Terry Pratchett was a very special writer.<br /><br />I tend to get a bit intimidated writing reviews about my favorite books, and I've gotten better about it over the years. There are so many thoughts and feelings swirling around my head towards this book. The first time I read it, it was eye opening. It changed the way I thought and feel about several different things and put some feelings I already had into perspective.<br /><br />This time, it serves more as a reminder. A hilarious reminder, well worth experiencing again and again. If asked what my favorite Terry Pratchett book is, I would say this one or Small Gods. They hit some of the same notes but don't hit the same ones quite as hard. Terry Pratchett is my favorite satirist and probably my favorite author. I tend to tell people to start with this book, as it's a nice stand alone novel. <br /><br />Really though, I think you can start the Discworld anywhere and be fine. I started with Thud, and I enjoyed it immensely(although, I did enjoy it more after learning more about Sam Vimes and his squad). This is one of my favorite books, in one of my favorite series, by one of my favorite authors. I think everyone should read it, and I have given at least three copies out that I can remember. I'm sure I'll read it many more times in my lifetime.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4709547090575737606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Lindsay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10326608-lindsay"><img alt="Lindsay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588746288p2/10326608.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1259302645?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsay" href="/user/show/10326608-lindsay">Lindsay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10326608-lindsay?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10326608-lindsay?shelf=humor">humor</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1259302645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14568184766073791850">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads' rel='nofollow noopener'>SpecFic Buddy Reads group</a> in 2019.<br/><br/>The small nation of Borogravia is at war, and has been at war, with just about everybody. The mad decrees of the Borogravian god Nuggan don't help. Polly needs her brother Paul back to help run the family's inn, so she disguises herself as a boy and joins up with the army. Her squad of recruits includes an Igor, a vampire, a troll, a few other traumatized young people and a tough-as-nails old sergeant named Jackrum, but they</span>
  <span id="freeText14568184766073791850" style="display:none">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads" rel="nofollow noopener">SpecFic Buddy Reads group</a> in 2019.<br /><br />The small nation of Borogravia is at war, and has been at war, with just about everybody. The mad decrees of the Borogravian god Nuggan don't help. Polly needs her brother Paul back to help run the family's inn, so she disguises herself as a boy and joins up with the army. Her squad of recruits includes an Igor, a vampire, a troll, a few other traumatized young people and a tough-as-nails old sergeant named Jackrum, but they may be just the group the country needs to survive.<br /><br />This is only my second time through for this one and I remember being unimpressed the first time around. Silly younger me. This is a masterpiece. Slightly different from most of the other Discworld books, but marvelously demonstrating just how far Pratchett's feminism and overall humanistic writing matured over the long time between the first Discworld books and this one. I could ramble incoherently for a long time on this, but instead I'll refer people to the last essay in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22887784.Pratchett_s_Women" title="Pratchett's Women by Tansy Rayner Roberts" rel="nofollow noopener">Pratchett's Women</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/599287.Tansy_Rayner_Roberts" title="Tansy Rayner Roberts" rel="nofollow noopener">Tansy Rayner Roberts</a> which deals exclusively with this novel. I will add a mention of Nadezhda Durova who feels like the inspiration of a major character in this book, particularly in her life after war.<br /><br />Original review, 4 stars, this time around bumped to 5.</span>

<div id="review_708385298" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/708385298" />
    <a title="Carly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4490040-carly"><img alt="Carly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1387424174p2/4490040.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<div id="review_1957451916" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1957451916" />
    <a title="YouKneeK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek"><img alt="YouKneeK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386369592p2/25923230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1957451916?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YouKneeK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YouKneeK" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek">YouKneeK</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6567847-monstrous-regiment">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=completed-series">completed-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1957451916" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1061693558491577499"><i>Monstrous Regiment</i> is the third book in the <i>Industrial Revolution</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. <br/><br/>The book starts off with our main character, Polly, transforming herself into Oliver. Yep, she’s chopping off her hair and dressing up like a boy so she can go and enlist in the military. She lives in a small country that’s always at war with its neighbors. Women here are not allowed to dress like men or fight. It is, along with many other things such as cats and chocolate, an Abomination to their god. <br/><br/>This</span>
  <span id="freeText1061693558491577499" style="display:none"><i>Monstrous Regiment</i> is the third book in the <i>Industrial Revolution</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. <br /><br />The book starts off with our main character, Polly, transforming herself into Oliver. Yep, she’s chopping off her hair and dressing up like a boy so she can go and enlist in the military. She lives in a small country that’s always at war with its neighbors. Women here are not allowed to dress like men or fight. It is, along with many other things such as cats and chocolate, an Abomination to their god. <br /><br />This <i>Industrial Revolution</i> book seemed a little different from the previous two because it didn’t introduce a revolutionary new industry. It was more of a war (or anti-war) book, and a book about prejudice and stereotypes. It did have some ties to the previous books though, mostly in the form of character cameos. I liked the story pretty well, and it had some good (if not very original) messages as well as some humor. It did get over-the-top ridiculous at some points, even for <i>Discworld</i>, particularly toward the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1061693558491577499" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_168847340" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168847340" />
    <a title="Shovelmonkey1" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1"><img alt="Shovelmonkey1" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391713268p2/3926982.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
